Preface
This master thesis has been conducted in coorperation with department of Wind Energy
located at the Mechanical Institute, Technical University of Denmark (DTU). Associate Pro-
fessor Christian Berggreen and Researcher Mohsen Rezaei, both from DTU, have been the
supervisors for this project. The company Fiberline Composites A/S has produced and de-
livered the composite material used in this project.
The duration of the project has been 7 months, including 2 month extension due to de-
liverence of composite materials. The project corresponds to 30 ECTS points.
Student: Mads Borgnæs

==PAGE:3==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Abstract
The increasing focus and demand of renewable energy have lead to increasing sizes of wind
turbines. This has caused increased aerodynamic and inertia loads of the wind turbine
blades which the blade root has to withstand. In this thesis, a modiﬁed (steel and compos-
ite adhesively bonded) wind turbine blade-root end bushing will be investigated through
experimental tests and numerical analysis using cohesive zone modeling (CZM).
The material stiﬀness properties of the uni-directional glass-ﬁber reinforced polymer (GFRP),
which was produced and delivered by Fiberline Composites A/S, were determined from
tensile and shear tests.
A J-integral theory for a multilayered Double Cantilever Beam-Uneven Bending Moment
(DCB-UBM) specimen were then used and modiﬁed to represent a bi-material specimen.
The energy release rate could then be obtained only as function of applied bending moment
during testing. The DCB-UBM tests were conducted using a developed hydraulic test-rig
with torsional actuators. Through these tests the critical energy release rates for diﬀerent
Mode-Mixities were measured and an analytical function were ﬁtted to the data points and
the Mode I and Mode II critical energy release rates could be extracted. For determining
the Mode-Mixity during DCB-UBM tests, a ﬁnite element (FE) model was developed. The
Mode-Mixity could then be directly determined through the reduced phase angle approach,
where the Mode I and Mode II energy release rate were obtained using the Virtual Crack
Closure Technique (VCCT). The Mode I and Mode II critical energy release rates were used
to deﬁne cohesive laws to be implemented for FE simulations.
Double shear lap (DSL) tensile tests were conducted and a FE model with CZM was cre-
ated. From these tests, the correct cohesive law deﬁned and later be implemented for the
root-end bushing FE models.
Pure tensile tests of modiﬁed bushings were conducted using a DTU developed multi-axial
test-rig. The tests proved, that the manufacturing of the bushings had been bad and could
not be compared with the developed FE models. However, two advanced bushing FE mod-
els (2D and 3D) were developed with use of CZM. The 2D model is intended to be used
in pure tensile loading condition and the 3D model is intended to be used in an eccentric
loading condition.
For further investigations, the interface in terms of cohesive laws should be further investi-
gated and the bushing FE models should be further updated to be able to conduct fatigue
simulations, that can be compared with actual fatigue tests.

Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
1
Introduction
Renewable energy and thereby also wind energy has gained more interest over the years.
Wind turbine farms are constructed - both onshore and oﬀshore and there is a constant
need to further develop, design, produce and install wind turbines in order to maximize
the energy output and minimize the overall cost. This has lead to increasing wind turbines
dimensions over the years which has also introduced larger loading of the diﬀerent com-
ponents.
For larger blades of a wind turbine, the external ﬂapwise and edgewise loadings becomes
larger but also the weight increases. These increasing loads, external and internal, intro-
duces higher axial forces and bending moments at the bolted connection between the hub
and the root-end of the blades. It is therefore required that this connection has enough
structural integrity sustain these loads which makes the connection crusial part of the wind
turbine blade design.
Fiberline Composites A/S is a danish company that has developed a solution of a wind
turbine blade root-end connection where the steel bushing is embedded to the composite
material (ﬁbers directly impregnated to steel). The interface strength between the steel and
composite is to this day determined by pure tensile tests of root-end bushing specimens. In
Figure 1.1 the root-end bushings produced by Fiberline Composites A/S is shown:
Figure 1.1: Picture of root-end connection (left) and picture of one root-end bushing connection
(right)1
The main goal is to simulate and predict the pull-out failure load of a root-end bushing spec-
imen through ﬁnite element (FE) analysis where the interface are modeled using bilinear
cohesive zone modeling (CZM). The reason for modeling the root-end bushing specimens
using cohesive elements is due to their ability to predict failure, both for ductile and brittle
interfaces. The bushing model will therefore be a further development of the one devel-
oped in [1]. The model will be able to simulate static eccentric- and pure tension loading.
For making a trustworthy and realistic FE model, several experimental tests and analyses
will be conducted. Digital Image Correlation (DIC) will be used for all experiments in this
project while all FE models and analyisis in this project are created with commercial soft-
ware program, ANSYS v. 17.2.
1https://ﬁberline.com/wind-turbine-components/blade-root-reinforcements
10
==PAGE:12==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
1.1
Outline of Project
From experimental test the material properties of the composite, which is a uni-directional
(UD) glass-ﬁber reinforced polymer (GFRP), be deﬁned. These tests will be done using
standard testing methods.
The interface strength between steel and GFRP will be investigated through Double Can-
tilever Beam-Uneven Bending Moment (DCB-UBM) tests, using a new hydraulic test-rig
designed at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). This will be a new approach com-
pared to DCB-UBM specimens loaded with wires and roller distances [2],[3]. A developed
multi-layered J-integral [4] will be used to measure the energy release rate of the interface.
The result obtained here will be used to deﬁne the cohesive zone elements for numerical
simulations. Numerical simulations using the Virtual Crack Closure Technique (VCCT)
method will be performed to determine the phase angle and thereby Mode-Mixity of the
DCB-UBM specimens, when loaded at diﬀerent moment ratios and conﬁgurations (posi-
tion of materials).
For understanding how the cohesive laws, extracted from the DCB-UBM tests work, in-
termediate tests will be performed. This will include static tensile tests of double shear lap
(DSL) specimens. The DSL tests failure mode and failure load will be compared with nu-
merical simulations using obtained cohesive laws.
The ﬁnal tests for this project will be root-end bushings. These tests will be conducted in a
DTU designed moment-test rig. The experimental tests will be compared with numerical
simulation of the root-end bushing. The project test progress plan is shown in Figure 1.2
and will be described in detail in the coming sections.
Figure 1.2: Step-by-step progress test plan for the project
11
==PAGE:13==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs

1.2
Modiﬁed Root-End Bushing
For this project, it was ﬁrst intended that bushings fabricated by Fiberline Composites A/S
should have been used and tested. To extract energy release rates and cohesive laws between
the GFRP and steel, Fiberline Composites A/S also had to manufacture the DCB-UBM and
DSL specimens, since it important that the interface between the two materials are constant
for all tested components for being able to generate a valid FE model. It was not possible
to get Fiberline Composites A/S to manufacture the DCB-UBM and DSL specimens and
because of this, it was decided to self-manufacture all test specimens for this project with use
of adhesive layer,which in this project has been Araldite 2015 [39]. In this project, bushings
with bolt size M20 have been used for testing.
12
==PAGE:14==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
2
Bi-material Energy Release Rate & Mode-Mixity
This section presents the main theoretical background used in order to determine the en-
ergy release rates and cohesive laws of the interface between GFRP and steel. By this point
it is not known if ﬁber bridging will occur or if the fracture between GFRP and steel can be
treated as a brittle, linear elastic fracture mechanic (LEFM) problem. Regardless of the type
of crack development (linear or non-linear) the purpose in this project is to describe and
treat the fracture process with use of bilinear cohesive laws, which is explained in Section
3.1.
2.1
Energy Release Rate, G
Griﬃth [7] introduced an energy-based criterion for a crack in a homogenous body. This
theory is based on two energy contributions, the strain energy, UE, at the crack tip and
surface energy, SE, which is based on the separation of atomic bonds of the material. In
Figure 2.1 the strain-, surface- and total energy are illustrated as a function of crack length
(two dimensional problem):
Figure 2.1: Surface energy, strain energy and total energy as function of crack length, a
From Figure 2.1 a crack becomes unstable when the strain energy overcomes the surface
energy:
dWE
da
= dUE
da + dSE
da = 0 ⇔−dUE
da = dSE
da
(2.1)
Irwin [8] deﬁned the energy release rate, G, which is a driving force for crack growth:
G = |dUE

(2.2)
Where crack propagation will take place when the critical energy release rate, Gc, is reached.
This is a material constant but can vary depending on the loading Mode of the cracked body.

2.2
Phase Angle and Mode-Mixity of Bimaterial
For a homogenous DCB-UBM specimen, pure Mode I and Mode II can be obtained by ap-
plying bending moments to the free ends as sketched in Figure 2.3:
Figure 2.3: Mode I & Mode II fracture condition for homogenous material
In this project, counter-clockwise bending will be treated as the positive direction. For a
homogenous DCB-UBM specimen, pure Mode I is obtained at a moment ratio, MR, of -
1 and Mode II is obtained at a moment ratio of +1. During the DCB-UBM tests in this
project, where the specimens consists of orthotropic GFRP and isotropic steel, Mixed-Mode
conditions will occur for the specimens, even at a moment ratio of 1 and -1. This is due to
the mismatch in stiﬀness and material properties and for determining the rate of Mode-
Mixity the reduced phase angle is used in this project, and will be simply refered to as
phase angle. There exists two forms of the phase angle; reduced- and full form [9]. The
diﬀerence between the two forms is that the full form has two extra terms containing an
oscillatory index, which the reduced form does not have. In practice, the reduced form
is much more intuitive, as it gives an understanding of the Mode-Mixity. A pure Mode I
condition is equal to a reduced phase angle of 0o and while a pure Mode II is equal to 90o.
Phase angles in between means the condition is exposed to a Mixed-Mode condition [10].
The phase angle can be determined based from the energy release rates from Mode I and
Mode II [14]:
Ψ = tan−1
"s
GII
GI
#
(2.3)
The interface crack between two isotropic elastic materials was investigated in [12]. Here
14
==PAGE:16==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
an analytic solution for the phase angle of an isotropic linear elastic bimaterial is given as:
Ψ = tan−1
λsin(ω)−cos(ω +γ)

In (2.2), λ is a loading combination factor, γ is an angle restricted as |γ| ≤π

The Dundurs’ parameters are deﬁned below:
αD = ζG (κ2 +1)−(κ1 +1)

(2.5)

For linear isotropic bi-material DCB-UBM specimens, phase angle can be determined an-
alytically using tables developed in [12] for the angle, ω(αD,βD,η). In this project the bi-
material DCB-UBM specimens will be made from steel and GFRP. Using these developed
tables to determine ω would provide misleading results since steel is a linear elastic isotropic
material and the GFRP linear elastic orthotropic (transverse isotropic) material. In [13] a nu-
merically method, Crack Surface Displacement Extrapolation (CSDE) method, was used to
extract the angle ω and energy-release rates, G, for bi-material and sandwich (tri-material)
specimens. The CSDE method has not been developed or used in this project to determine
the phase angle of the bi-material DCB-UBM specimens.
In this project, to determine the energy release rates, GI and GII, another numerical method
will be used in this project, the Virtual Crack Closure Technique (VCCT). The VCCT is a
method that calculates the energy release rates under the assumption that energy used to
extend the crack (open the surface) is equal to the energy that is needed for the surface to
close [15]. The DCB-UBM specimen is modeled in 2D and a crack front node is deﬁned right
a the crack tip. The VCCT calculates the energy release rates in a 2D analysis. The energy
release rates are calculated based on the relative displacements between crack surfaces, ∆u
and ∆v. Energy release rates are found through calculation of the work with use of the
reaction forces at the deﬁned crack tip front node and the relative displacements of the
surfaces. For a 2D case the formulas are given as [40]:
GI = 1
2
Ry∆v
∆a
,
GII = 1
2
Rx∆u
∆a
(2.9)

the Mode I interfacial toughness, GIc:

2.3
J-Integral
The J-integral is a path independent integral for elastic materials that is used to determine
energy release rate and was introduced in [17]. The J-integral is deﬁned as:
J =
Z
Γ
Wdy −σijnj
∂ui
∂x dS
(2.19)
The theory was developed based on a 2D body with a notch/crack and with no external
forces acting on the body, see Figure 2.5:
Figure 2.5: Notch in 2D deformation ﬁeld (stresses only depends on x and y) of a homogenous
material
In Figure 2.5, the path, Γ, is surrounding the crack while σij is the stress tensor. The multi-
plication of the stress tensor and the corresponding outward normal, nj, yields the traction
vector, T. In (2.19) the integration along the path is done from the lower crack surface to
the upper crack surface (counter-clockwise) by inﬁnitesimal steps, dS. The displacement
vector is denoted u. The J-integral also uses the strain energy density, W:
W =
Z ϵ
0
σij ·dϵij
(2.20)

nous DCB-UBM specimen having analytical solutions for the J-integral is shown.

2.5
Cohesive Law Model
The interface between the steel and GFRP will be simulated using cohesive zone modelling.
There exist diﬀerent cohesive law models such as bilinear, trilinear and exponential. This
section will describe which is used and how it is used in this project for the numerical anal-
ysis. To limit the scope of the project the focus has been to analyze the root-end bushing
19
==PAGE:21==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
using cohesive zone interface elements with a bilinear cohesive law. In Figure 2.8 the bilin-
ear cohesive law is illustrated for both separation (Mode I dominant) and traction (Mode II
dominant).
Figure 2.8: Deﬁnition of bilinear traction-separation law for Mode I (left) and deﬁnition of bilinear
traction-separation law for Mode II (right)
Depending on the fracture type, diﬀerent approaches can be used to deﬁne the shape of the
cohesive traction-separation curve.
2.5.1
Measured Critical Energy Release Rates
Before conducting the DCB-UBM experiments it is expected that ﬁber bridging will not oc-
cur. This type of failure would be interfacial cracking. It is expected that the pre-crack will
start propagating through the interface between the two adherends. Linear elastic fracture
mechanics (LEFM) will be applicable if failure process zone (FPZ) is much smaller than
smallest specimen dimension (height, H) [5]. A small FPZ will lead to a energy based frac-
ture [19],[20]. In order simulate LEFM using cohesive zone modelling/cohesive laws, the
traction stresses needs to be relatively high compared to the cohesive separation length. An
indication of wheather the failure is energy or stress based can be determined by a nominal
cohesive length which is deﬁned for Mode I and Mode II as [21]:
ζI = GIc ·Ex
σc2
n
,
ζII = GIIc ·Ex
σc2
t
(2.24)
2.5.2
Partial Diﬀerentiation of JR
LEFM is not applicable for a large FPZ, which could occur during ﬁber bridging, plasticity
and friction. A large FPZ is usually the case if the process zone is larger than smallest
specimen dimension (e.g. height or pre-crack length) occurs when the failure process zone
is large only in the crack length direction [5]. Assume bridging occurs behind the crack tip,
the fracture resistance as function of pre-crack tip opening would take the form illustrated
in Figure 2.9 [4]:
20
==PAGE:22==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Figure 2.9: J as function of pre-crack tip opening
In Figure 2.9 there is a linear part up to J0 and then followed by a non-linear part between
until a steady-state fracture resistance is reached, Jss. For brittle fracture the non-linear part
does not occur, and in theory the fracture resistance will reach J0, which corresponds to the
critical strain energy release rate, and follow the dotted curve. Making a line integration
around the bridging zone normal and tangential stresses the total fracture resistance of the
crack and bridging zone becomes:
JR =

2.6
Double Shear Lap (DSL)
In this project, it has been chosen to test GFRP-steel DSL specimens to investigate the lo-
cation of failure and to verify the extracted cohesive laws. The brief theory provided here
and the test procedure in Section 4.5 are inspired by the work conducted in [22] and the
same methodology will is used in this project. A sketch of a DSL specimen with dimension
deﬁnitions is shown in Figure 2.10:
Figure 2.10: Illustration of Double Shear Lap (DSL) test specimen with dimension notation
21
==PAGE:23==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Standardized test procedures exist for Single Shear Lap (SSL) joints, such as ASTM D1002,
but usually the SSL specimens are chosen if the adherends are of same material. The rea-
son for choosing a DSL-type specimen was due to the mismatch in stiﬀness of the two ad-
herends (GFRP and steel). A SSL-type specimen consisting of bimaterial with diﬀerent stiﬀ-
ness’ would lead to a Mixed-Mode failure condition - and not the sought Mode II failure
[23],[24],[25]. The DSL specimens will be tested until failure, which will then provide a fail-
ure load. From this failure load the interface shear strength can be calculated by dividing
with the area:
τavg,DSL =
Pf
2wDSL ·LOL
(2.27)

failure mode and where the crack is is expected to initiate and propagate from. A non-linear
expression for shear stress along a bimaterial bondline has been developed [26]:

gives information about the location of maximum shear stress along the bondline. When ξ
is larger than 1 the peak shear stresses are located in the middle of the joint. The expression
for ξ is purely determined on the mismatch of material stiﬀness’ and thicknesses:
ξ =
E ·tsteel

The GFRP material properties needs to be determined ﬁrst in order to conclude the size of ξ,

ξ-value higher than 1. Therefore it is expected for the maximum shear stress to be located in
the middle of the joint which will lead to crack initiation and propagation from the middle

3
FE Models
Throughout this project diﬀerent FE models have been developed. The models developed
are listed below:
• DCB-UBM Model (2D)
• DSL Intermediate Model (2D)
• M20 Bushing Model (2D)
• M20 Bushing Model (3D)
All FE models are created in ANSYS through APDL-scripts. This makes it easy for the user
to modify the models regarding geometry, materials, meshing and loading. As described
earlier, the main goal of this project is be able to predict the pull-out strength of the root-end
bushing specimens using FE analysis. To validate the M20 bushing FE-models, experiments
are carried out but also an intermediate (DSL) FE model is developed to verify that the frac-
ture process between the steel and GFRP behave as supposed to. The interface between
steel and GFRP, for the DSL Model and M20 bushing models, are modeled using interface
cohesive elements with attached bilinear cohesive law.
All FE models are described in detail in the sections where the corresponding experimental
tests are explanied. Experimental set-up for the individual mechaincal test will be given
and then the corresponding FE model will be presented. The results from experiments will
be presented together with numerical results and further discussed.
3.1

of kI, which is the normal cohesive stiﬀness (undamaged). When the normal displacement

energy release rates for Mode I and Mode II (GIc and GIIc). With use of trigonometry GIc
and GIIc can be calculated as:
GIc = 1

(3.8)
GIIc = 1

(3.9)
By working with cohesive interface elements in ANSYS and based on the work carried out in
[18] it is concluded that it is not possible to describe a mixed mode cohesive law as function
of the phase angle:
Gc = Gc (Ψ) ⇒not possible
(3.10)
3.2
Cohesive Interface Elements in ANSYS
Depending on the analysis type (2D or 3D) diﬀerent types of elements are used. For the 3D
bushing models a solid brick element, SOLID185, is used to model the structural materi-
als. It is an 8-node linear element having three translational degrees of freedom (UX, UY
and UZ) at each node. For the 3D models the interface zone is modeled using the ANSYS
prescribed cohesive zone element, INTER205, which is a 8-node linear interface elements.
INTER205 also has no midside nodes, see Figure 3.3 which much be the case if it has to act
as adhesive using SOLID185 as the number of nodes need to match. The full description of
the elements can be found in [42],[43].
Figure 3.3: INTER205 w. global- and nodal coordinate system.
INTER205 also have three translational degrees of freedom (UX, UY and UZ). The behavior
of the cohesive interface elements is dependent on 6 parameters, which are listed in Table

4
Experimental Work and Numerical Analysis
This section describes all the tests that were conducted throughout this project. For every
test, data is presented, discussed and concluded. The experimental tests for this project
include material characterization tests of the GFRP-composite material, Mixed Mode frac-
ture testing of the GFRP-Steel bi-material specimens, intermediate tests (DSL-specimens)
of GFRP-Steel interface failure and full scale pull-out strength test (tension and bending)
of modiﬁed M20 root-end bushing specimens. All tests were conducted at the mechanical
testing facility in Building 119 at DTU, Kongens Lyngby. Digital Image Correlation (DIC)
was used for all tests to obtain data, which were later post-processed.
4.1
Adhesive Layer
The adhesive layer for the DCB-UBM, DSL and bushing specimens have been manufac-
tured in way, so the approximate thickness is 0.3 mm which is a thin bondline between the
two adherends, GFRP and steel. The adhesive for this project has been chosen as Araldite
2015. This is done to ’simulate’ the embedded steel bushing of an actual pultruded root-end
bushing specimen. For the DCB-UBM and DSL specimens, the manufacturing process and
control of the thickness were easier, due to the rectangular shapes of the adherends, com-
pared to the bushings. The main diﬃculty was to be ensure constant thickness of the whole
adhesive layer. The manufacturing process for DCB-UBM, DSL and bushings are given in
Section 4.3.2, 4.5.1 and 4.6.1, respectively.
According to [28] there are mainly three ways to numerically simulate the adhesive layer:
1 Adhesive layer modeled as an interface between the adherends present.
2 Adhesive layer modeled as elastoplastic continuum with material- and fracture pa-
rameters.
3 Adhesive layer modeled as an interface with thickness and a cohesive law.
For the numerical analysis’ in this project the adhesive layer will be modeled as method
1; interface between the present adherends. In Figure 4.1 an illustration of the diﬀerence
between method 1 and method 3 is shown:
29
==PAGE:31==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Figure 4.1: Cross-section of FE-model with adhesive solid elements present (top) and cross-section
of FE-model with cohesive elements to represent the interface between steel and GFRP (bottom)
An estimation of the shear stiﬀness of the interface can be approximated with use of the
shear stiﬀness for Araldite 2015 [39] and thickness of layer:
kII = Gad
tad
= 900 MPa
0.3 mm = 3000 MPa/mm
(4.1)
It was during experiments and investigations of force-displacement curves found that the
appropriate shear stiﬀness of the cohesive elements was 900 MPa/mm. This diﬀerence in
stiﬀness could be because of deviations of the adhesive thickness during manufacturing
and also the actual shear stiﬀness of the Araldite 2015 glue could deviate from the 900 MPa.
In [29] the connection and cohesive laws between aluminium alloys was investigated using
diﬀerent types of adhesives. In [29] a shear stiﬀness of Araldite 2015 was measured to 500-
1000 MPa. The stiﬀness of 900 MPa/mm in this project is therefore assumed to be valid.
4.2
Material Characterization Tests
Fiberline Composites A/S has manufactured and delivered all the GFRP composite material
for this project. The solid GFRP material has been tested for certain mechanical stiﬀness
properties, as Fiberline Composites A/S has changed the glass-ﬁber type since a previous
master thesis [1] of the root-end bushing was conducted. Fiberline Composites has stated
that with their modiﬁcation of glass-ﬁber type they expect the new longitudinal stiﬀness to
be less than it was previously. The material characterization tests in this project included
tensile and shear tests of certain directions of the GFRP material.

4.2.1.2
GFRP (Composite)
The GFRP material is manufactured using a production line pultrusion process. This is a
method to construct continuous composite proﬁles with a constant cross-section. First the
reinforcement (ﬁbers) are positioned correctly and pulled through a processing equipment
where the ﬁbres are impregnated with the matrix. The last step of the process is cutting
the material in the material in correct dimensions, which is done by a saw. The pultrusion
process is illustrated in Figure 4.3 [38].
31
==PAGE:33==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Figure 4.3: Pultrusion process at Fiberline Composites A/S2
The GFRP material for this project is a uni-directional (UD) composite. The ﬁbers are placed
in one direction (longitudinal) and then impregnated into the matrix. An illustration of a
GFRP block is shown in Figure 4.4 below:
Figure 4.4: UD GFRP Composite ﬁber orientation
From Figure 4.4 the ﬁbers are placed parallel to the x-axis and are perpendicular to the y-
and z-axis’ (transverse axis’). The GFRP composite material properties thereby has three
orthogonal planes of symmetry and has 9 independent material properties; elastic moduli
(x, y and z-direction), shear moduli (xy-, yz- and xz-plane) and Poisson’s ratio (xy-, yz- and
xz-plane). These are shown in the compliance matrix seen in (4.3).

4.2.2

The GFRP composite material was cut at the DTU Risø campus according to the drawing
seen in Appendix G.1. Five tensile tests and data post-processing were performed accord-
ing to ASTM D3039/3039M [34]. A 100 kN hydraulic MTS load was used for testing.
To measure the surface strains of the specimens under tensile loading, two 12 megapix-
els DIC cameras with 75 mm. lens were used. The data were extracted using the soft-
ware from by Geometrical Optical Measurement (GOM). The cameras were placed and po-
sitioned based on the specimen size. This was done with use of a adjustable and moveable
tripod. The lighting were conﬁgured so it was possible for the cameras to detect the painted
surface. The tensile test set-up is shown in Figure 4.6.
Figure 4.6: Tensile test set-up with DIC

4.2.3
GFRP Shear Test
To determine the shear modulus in the xy- and xz-plane ASTM D7078/7078M [35] was fol-
lowed. The V-notch shear specimens were cut and manufactured at the DTU Risø campus
according to the drawing listed in Appendix G.1. Five V-notch specimens were tested. The
tests were performed using the same 100 kN hydraulic load cell as was used during ten-
sile test. Before testing the shear specimens they were painted with a black-dotted pattern
for the upcoming DIC measurements. The sizes of shear specimens were a little smaller
than the tensile specimens but the area of measuring interest was approximately the same.
Therefore the same DIC set-up could be used and a re-calibration could be omitted. The
calibration deviation was 0.033 and is shown in Appendix A.2 and the DIC set-up parame-
ters are listed in Table 4.3.
To conduct the shear tests, additional grips were inserted to the hydraulic load cell. In
Figure 4.10 a DIC-painted shear specimen is shown during installation.

The failure mode shown in Figure 4.14 is an acceptable failure mode according to [35] and
has the failure code HGN. The code describes the failure type, area and location in that
order and is deﬁned as:

• G = Gauge section
• N = Between notches
The horizontal cracks are marked with red circles in Figure 4.14 and high strains/cracks
occurs in the gauge section area and the location is between the notches. This indicates that
the shear tests were correctly conducted. In Appendix A.6 the shear strain at failure for all
ﬁve shear specimens are shown.

4.3
DCB-UBM Test
After the material properties were deﬁned, the DCB-UBM test could be initiated. Due to
manufacturing diﬃculties only ﬁve DCB-UBM specimens were tested, each one at their
phase angle, meaning no phase angle was tested multiple times.
The DCB-UBM tests included both numerical analysis for deﬁning the phase angle at dif-
ferent moment ratios at diﬀerent conﬁgurations/position of materials. The tests were per-
formed using a using a DTU-designed test-rig with two torsional load cells of 565 Nm at-
tached to actuators on each arm. An illustration of test set-up is shown in Figure 4.15:
Figure 4.15: Illustration of DCB-UBM test
The test-rig has been constructed in way so the inserted specimens will only be subjected to
pure bending moments, M1 and M2. This has been achieved by mounting the actuators on
frictionless rollers. The specimens were allowed to move in the translational x-direction by
rollers [36]. The J-integral was calculated based on the formula given in (2.22) as function
of the applied moments at each arm. DIC were used to track the sliding and opening at the
crack tip during loading.
4.3.1
Phase Angle Analysis from VCCT
The phase angle analyses based on VCCT described in Section 2.2 was used to determine
the phase angles at diﬀerent moment ratios with diﬀerent conﬁgurations/positions of ad-
herends (top/bottom). There are six diﬀerent load conﬁgurations which has been analyzed
using VCCT. The six conﬁgurations are shown in Figure 4.16
41
==PAGE:43==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Figure 4.16: Load case conﬁgurations of DCB-UBM
The moment ratio is deﬁned as M1/M2. The used DCB-UBM test-rig in this project has been
constructed and tuned to have a master arm (M1) and a slave arm (M2). The user gives the
moment ratio as an input, which must be equal or larger than unity (-1 & 1). Looking at load
case conﬁguration 1 and 4 they look similar. However, this is only the case for a moment
ratio of -1. The cases diﬀer from each other when the absolute value of the moment ratio
increases (-2, -3 etc.). This is because the DCB-UBM specimen is categorized as a bi-material.
The DCB-UBM FE model has been constructed using the parametric APDL script developed
during this project. The script can be seen in Appendix F.1. The model and analysis are
described in the following sections.
4.3.1.1
Geometry
In Figure 4.17 the geometry and dimensions are shown for the DCB-UBM model.

4.3.1.2
Materials
The material properties implemented in the model are taken from Table 4.7. In the VCCT
analysis no cohesive elements are present. This is due to the fact the VCCT-method is a
linear analysis.
4.3.1.3
Elements and Meshing
The DCB-UBM model has been modeled in 2D with PLANE182 elements with use of plane
strain extrapolation due to the 40 mm. width of the specimen. These elements have two
translational degrees of freedom at each node, UX and UY. The area of interest in this anal-
ysis is the crack-tip area and therefore the main focus is to develop a model with high in-
tensity mesh at that speciﬁc area. This is achieved by dividing the lines with ratio-spacing
of 150, meaning the line divisions becomes smaller close to the crack tip. An overview of
the mesh is shown in Figure 4.18:
43
==PAGE:45==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Figure 4.18: Meshed DCB-UBM model. Detailed overview of mesh intensity at crack-tip
The element side length at the crack tip is approximately 0.029 mm. The one node a the
crack tip has been deﬁned as the crack tip node. The energy release rate for Mode I and
Mode II are extracted from this node. A mesh sensitivity analysis has been carried out, see
Figure 4.19.
Number of elements [-]

4.3.1.4
Boundary Conditions and Loads
The loading of each beam in the DCB-UBM model is done by contact constraining the nodes
at the end of top beam part to a key point and the nodes at the end of the bottom beam part
44
==PAGE:46==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
to another key point. The nodes constrained at each beam corresponds to the length of the
doubler which is 40 mm. The applied load is done by applying a moment to each of the
keypoints (M1 and M2). The user deﬁnes the moment of the top adherend (M1), in Nm, and
deﬁnes a moment ratio, MR, which can be negative or positive depending on the load con-
dition which has to be investigated. Since the model is a plane strain analysis the moment
load input is transformed to be moment per width. The model has been displacement con-
strained in the y-direction at the end of the specimen, which simulates the roller supports.
An x-direction constrain has been introduced to avoid rigid body motion. An illustration of
the meshed FE-model with applied boundary conditions can be seen in Figure 4.20 where
the horizontal red lines indicate the contact constrained nodes which are used to distribute
the moments. The analysis conducted in this project has been done using M1 = 50 Nm.
Figure 4.20: Boundary conditions and applied loads
The two adherents are merged together and no contact constraints has been applied be-
tween the two. Unrealistic loading must therefore be taken into account when doing the
VCCT-analysis. In Figure 4.21 an accepted loading condition and an unacceptable loading
condition are shown:
Figure 4.21: Conﬁguration 2 at MR = 5 (left) and at MR = 6 (right)
In the right picture the two substrates overlap, which is not a valid result when doing real
experimental results. Because GFRP are less stiﬀthan steel, loading conﬁguration 5 is not
considered during the VCCT-analysis where the moment ratio goes from 1 to 10. In this
range the GFRP would always press against the steel, which has to be avoided during test-
ing.
45
==PAGE:47==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017

4.3.2
Manufacturing of DCB-UBM specimens
The adherends for the DCB-UBM specimens were cut at the DTU Risø campus according
to the drawings in Appendix G.2 and Appendix G.3. Four M5 threaded holes were drilled
so the doubler inserts could be connected to the ﬁnal manufactured specimens. The steel-
GFRP DCB-UBM specimens were manufactured using Araldite 2015 adhesive as connec-
47
==PAGE:49==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
tion between the two adherends. A layer of 0.3 mm. were used to simulate a thin bondline
and still making sure a adhesive were present after applied pressure. The thickness of ad-
hesive was controlled using 0.3 mm. wires in between the steel and GFRP. The wires were
placed at places to avoid interference with pre-crack and the predicted crack growth. An
illustration of the procedure for making one DCB-UBM specimen is shown in Figure 4.23.
Figure 4.23: Steel adherend (top) and GFRP adherend (bottom) manufacturing process for DCB-
UBM specimens
Before applying adhesive and connecting the two parts, they were subjected to a thorough
surface treatment and cleaning. Both the steel and GFRP were grinded on one side (ad-
hesive side) using abrasive sand paper #60 in random directions. This was done in order
to make a rough surface that the adhesive could stick to. The grinding was done using a
machine with installed #60 sand paper band, see Figure 4.24.
Figure 4.24: Steel during grinding (left) and grinded surface (right)
After grinding, the adherends were cleaned multiple times before the gluing process to
make sure all dirt was removed from the surface. The cleaning process was done using
acetone. A measured pre-crack distance of 70 mm. was marked on each GFRP pieces and
ﬁlm was placed in that area. This was done to simulate the pre-crack as the adhesive can not
stick to ﬁlm. Before the steel and GFRP could be connected, a simple ’alignment-groove’
48
==PAGE:50==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
was constructed on a rigid steel table. The groove was made out of steel beams and served
to align the specimens during clamping and to protect the table from adhesive. In Figure
4.25 the GFRP pieces with attached ﬁlms are placed in the designed table groove.
Figure 4.25: GFRP adherends with ﬁlm for pre-crack placed in designed groove
The clamping of specimens were done using a steel bar, long enough press on all 5 speci-
mens, on top of the specimens and applying pressure using multiple grips. The grips were
placed in a way to simulate a uniform distributed loading across all specimens, see Figure
4.26.
Figure 4.26: Clamped set-up (left) and close-up view at ﬁnished DCB-UBM cross-section
49
==PAGE:51==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
4.3.3
Test Set-Up: DCB-UBM
The DCB-UBM tests were performed at DTU in the lab of building 119. The previous de-
scribed DCB-UBM test machine is shown in Figure 4.27. Each beam of the DCB-UBM spec-
imens were connected to the doubler inserts and moment arms by 4 M5 bolts.
Figure 4.27: DCB-UBM Test-rig
The machine was controlled through inputs given to moment arm 1, master arm. Moment
arm 2 then acted as a slave arm. The inputs given to the machine was moment ratio and
direction of rotation. It was then possible to test all conﬁgurations given in Figure 4.16. The
machine could only apply moment ratios of larger equal or larger than unity. The purpose
for these tests was to estimate the Mode I and Mode II cohesive laws of the GFRP-steel
interface, which then could be used as input parameters for FE models. During loading of
the specimens, the displacements at the pre-crack tip were tracked using 12 Megapixel DIC.
Due to the diﬃculties regarding specimen manufacturing, only ﬁve DCB-UBM specimens
were tested and measured with DIC. A diﬀerent loading condition (phase angle) was tested
each time. A picture of the test set-up is shown in Figure 4.28
50
==PAGE:52==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Figure 4.28: Test set-up for DCB-UBM testing
The DIC calibration ﬁle can be seen in Appendix B.1 and the set-up properties are listed in
Table 4.11. The cameras were positioned above the specimens and tilted in order to track the
crack growth (opening and sliding) of the pre-crack. The speed of all tests was performed
at 1 deg/min.

The moment-rotation curves for all ﬁve tested specimens can be seen in Appendix B.2 The
applied moment development shown in Figure 4.30 diﬀers compared to the moment devel-
opment in Figure 4.31. For specimen #2, a plateau is reached in the beginning of the loading
history. This plateau does not occur when loading a specimen with a moment ratio of 1/-1.
This moment development containing a plateau in the beginning of the loading history, is
believed to be an issue regarding the ﬁxture at the rollers. If the specimen was not locked
tightly against the rollers, the specimen would be free to rotate at a constant moment. At
some point the specimen would lock it self to towards the rollers, and thereby again making
the moments increase.
Also, the rollers are kept in place at each side of the test-rig with two bolts at each side
thereby ﬁxing the rollers by friction. At diﬀerent loading conﬁgurations and moment ratios,
the rollers could experience very high pressure loads, thereby making them slide, which
would provide a plateau to the load curve. During the ﬁrst DCB-UBM tests, it was ﬁrst be-
lieved, that the ﬁrst plateau was the steady-state area of th crack propagation, but through
more tests it was discovered that the steady-state occured at higher bending moments.
A load drop is also visible in Figure 4.30. That is believed to be because of the specimen sur-
face not being perfectly smooth, when moving across the rollers. In Figure 4.32 the moment
is plotted as function of the pre-crack total opening for specimen #2 in Table 4.12:

ductile. Before conducting the ﬁve DCB-UBM experiments with use of DIC, dummy tests

4.4
Cohesive Law for FE-modeling
From the DCB-UBM test the critical energy release rates for pure Mode I and pure Mode
II were determined by ﬁtting a function to the tested Mixed-Mode critical energy release
rates. From the ﬁtted interfacial fracture toughness curve the critical energy release rates
from pure Mode I and pure Mode II are given as:
GIc = 550 J/m2
,
GIIc = 1740.8 J/m2
(4.14)
These critical energy release values are in the same range as ones found in [28], [29]. The
crack propagation indicated that there was no sign of ﬁber bridging and that the fracture
process zone was rather small. The crack propagation and the interface of the bi-material
was therefore decided to be treated as being brittle. Because of this and also uncertainty of
measured crack opening and sliding partial diﬀerentiation of the fracture resistance curves
has there not been used to extract cohesive laws. Instead, the theory provided in Section
2.5.1 has been used. To simulate brittle fracture, the nominal cohesive zone length (ζI and
ζII) has to be small. This means that the maximum nominal cohesive zone length should be
around the minimum dimension of the specimen. In Table 4.14 and Table 4.15 ﬁve diﬀerent
bilinear cohesive law shapes for Mode I and Mode II have been proposed.
Table 4.14: Diﬀerent shapes of Mode I cohesive laws

For application, the diﬀerence between the two cohesive laws shown in Figure 4.38 is that #4
is mainly stress controlled and #2 is energy controlled. Because the DCB-UBM tests showed
brittle fracture it is expected that cohesive law #1 and cohesive law #2 (small nominal cohe-
sive zone lengths) will simulate the crack propagation best when doing numerical analysis.
4.4.1
DCB-UBM Numerical Analysis Vs. Experiments
The cohesive law parameters deﬁned in Table 4.14 and Table 4.15 are assigned to the cohe-
sive interface elements for FE-modeling. To validate that the critical energy release values
are within a correct range and that the shape of cohesive laws are correct, the DCB-UBM
FE model described in Section 4.3.1 are again used but with cohesive interface elements
implemented between the two materials. One load conﬁguration is investigated and that is
conﬁguration 6 (GFRP on top) with a moment ratio of +2. The moments are applied in the
negative direction (clockwise) for both beams. For all simulations, a moment load of -140
Nm and -70 Nm have been applied to arm 1 and arm 2, respectively. A summary of results
is given in Table 4.16.

It is seen in Table 4.16 that the load cases do not reach 1 second, which means the FE simu-

reached. The numerical simulations does not converge when reaching the certain moments
which is interpreted as the crack starts to become unstable and will run through the inter-
face. To be certain that this is the case for non-convergence, an analysis has been conducted
with Mode I and Mode II cohesive laws #2 with critical energy release rates reduced by 90%
(same nominal cohesive zone lengths). That gave the following results:

Comparing these numerical simulations with the experimentally obtained moment plots of
the similar cases seen in Appendix B.2 it is seen that the cohesive parameters implemented
in FE-simulations predicts very well at which load the crack will start to propagate. This
indicates, that the obtained critical energy release rates for Mode I and Mode II and the
used cohesive law works. However, it can not from these analysis be concluded which of
the suggested cohesive laws is the best to simulate the interface. It can only be concluded
that the obtained critical release energies are correct. All ﬁve cohesive laws will be used for
comparing and analyzing double shear lap (DSL) specimens. Since the fracture of the DCB-
UBM were brittle, the fracture of DSL specimens are expected to be brittle and cohesive law
#1 or #2 are expected to be the most correct ones since their nominal cohesive zone lengths
are small compared to the other suggested cohesive laws.
4.5
DSL (Intermediate) Test
The idea for testing Double Shear Lab (DSL) specimens, see Figure 4.41, is to compare the
failure load and failure type (debonding) with a DSL FE-model where cohesive interface
elements are present between the steel and GFRP adherends. These tests and analysis will
then be evaluated and compared to see if the failure load and type are somehow alike. The
conclusion from the DSL analysis will help determine if the FE-plots for the bushing can be
trusted. When testing the bushings it is only possible to obtain a failure load (potentially)
and track the strains at the surface using DIC. The pull-out tests does not give any informa-
tion about failure zone at the bondline inside of the root-end bushings. From the DSL tests
61
==PAGE:63==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
it is possible to see this interaction. The DSL tests act as an ’intermediate’ test that tries to
connect the obtained cohesive laws from DCB-UBM tests to prove that interface between
steel and GFRP behaves as it should in the bushing.
Figure 4.41: Double Shear Lap (DSL) test specimen
The tests were performed using a 100 kN hydraulic MTS load cell. The length of the GFRP
pieces are 80 mm., thickness is 10 mm. and width is 40 mm. The steel parts have a length of
150 mm., thickness of 10 mm. and width of 40 mm. The dimensions of the DSL specimens
have then been chosen so the steel will always be inside the in the linear elastic zone - even
at load of 100 kN.
4.5.1
Manufacturing of DSL specimens
The DSL specimens were self-manufactured at DTU Risø campus. The composite and steel
adherends for the DSL specmimen were cut according to drawings in Appendix G.4 and
G.5, respectively. In order to create an approximately identical interface as the DCB-UBM
specimens, the same approach was used.
The steel bars were grinded in mulitple random directions on the surface at both sides,
using a grinding machine with abrasive paper #60 installed. The same was done for the
GFRP pieces, but only on the side which was later connected to the steel. All steel bars and
GFRP pieces were then surface cleaned with acetone. Just before applying the adhesive
(Araldite 2015), the surfaces were again cleaned with acetone. To control the thickness of
the adhesive layer, 0.3 mm steel wires were inserted in the middle of the GFRP overlap, see
Figure 4.42 below:
Figure 4.42: Exploded view of DSL specimen
In Figure 4.43 a DSL specimen is shown after assembly. The steel bars were supported by
nuts (see bottom right corner) in order to keep them straight during clamping.
62
==PAGE:64==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Figure 4.43: DSL specimen assembled before clamping
The steel wires were placed in the middle of the overlap to have as less inﬂuence on the
fracture initiation, as the crack would develop from the middle or edge. In Figure 4.44 the
ﬁve DSL specimens are shown ready for clamping. To get the best eﬀect of the adhesive, the
specimens were put under pressure for 24 hours. Putting the specimens on a rigid steel table
with two steel beams acting as walls did this. A large steel beam, covering all 5 specimens,
was put on top. The walls and top were then pressed/clamped to the specimens by using
grips. The grips were placed to approximately simulate a uniform distributed pressure.
Figure 4.44: 5 DSL specimens before clamping
4.5.2
Test Set-Up: DSL
The DSL tests were performed at DTU in the lab of building 119. The test set-up is shown
in Figure 4.45.

Five DSL specimen were static load tested until failure. The specimens were painted with a
dotted pattern as the surface displacements were measured using DIC. The DIC consisted
of two 12 Megapixels cameras with 75 mm. lenses. The DIC set-up properties are given in
Table 4.20 and the calibration ﬁle is shown in Appendix C.1. The purpose for tests was to
locate the crack initiation and also be able to plot experimental force-displacements curves.
A virtual extensometer was installed along the full specimen length using DIC. The force-
displacement curves shown in Figure 4.49 are generated using the virtual extensometers
and the load output from the load cell. Before each test, a couple of pictures were taken in
order to check that the noise-level was acceptable.

4.5.3
FE-Model of DSL Intermediate Test (2D)
This section gives a description of the created FE-model of the DSL test. The model has been
created using an APDL-script and the code can be seen in Appendix F.2. The DSL specimen
tests results are in this section compared with the numerically obtained results from ANSYS.
The cohesive laws extracted from the DCB-UBM are implemented to the model with use of
cohesive zone elements between the adherends.
4.5.3.1
Geometry
Since the DSL specimen is symmetric in the xz- and yz-plane only 1/4-part of the specimen
has been modeled. This reduces the number of nodes, which reduces the CPU time. In Fig-
64
==PAGE:66==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
ure 4.46 the meshed FE model is shown with dimension deﬁnitions and global orientation.
Figure 4.46: Deﬁnitions of 2D DSL FE geometry
The dimensions are listed in Table 4.21. Note the steel thickness is only 5 mm. This is due
to the symmetry condition. The length of steel is length from the middle of the specimen
to the grip edge. This dimension was measured for every test and the average value was 85
mm.
Table 4.21: Dimensions of FE DSL specimen
Parameter
Symbol
Size
Overlap length
LOL
40 mm
Length of steel
Lsteel
85 mm
Thickness of steel
tsteel
5 mm
Thickness of GFRP
tGFRP
10 mm
4.5.3.2
Materials
The material properties implemented in the model are taken from Table 4.7. From the DCB-
UBM tests it was found that the Mode I and Mode II critical energy release rates for this
manufactured bi-material should be 550 J/m2 and 1740.8 J/m2, respectively. In order for
the cohesive elements to represent the in adhesive layer, the stiﬀness of the cohesive ele-
ments must be modeled in order for the system to have a correct stiﬀness (slope of force-
displacement curve). A stiﬀness of 900 MPa/mm was found to be suﬃcient. In Figure 4.38
the cohesive law for Mode II is shown, since the DSL is a proposed pure Mode II condition.
4.5.3.3
Elements and Meshing
The structural solids, steel and GFRP, are modeled using PLANE182-elements and due to
the thickness of the specimen plane strain is used. The element has 4-node and two transla-
tional degrees of freedom, UX and UY, at each node. The cohesive elements that have been
implemented are INTER202. In Figure 4.47 the meshed model is shown:
65
==PAGE:67==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Figure 4.47: Meshed model. High intensity mesh at bondline
The length of the interface elements should, as a rule of thumb, be maximum the size of the
critical separation value, δc
t. The developed APDL-script for this model, see Appendix F.2,
is able to simply adjust the mesh length at the bondline by one parameter, n. This parameter
is proportional to the bondline element length. Setting n equal to 1 gives an elements length
of δc
t.
4.5.3.4
Boundary Conditions and Loads
As stated earlier, symmetry condtions has been applied in order to reduce CPU time. Since
the steel bars are separated by ﬁlm and therefore not connected, a gap of 0.2 mm. is made
and the symmetry of the yz-plane is only applied to the GFRP nodes. The loading is done
by deﬁning a displacment in the negative x-direction, see Figure 4.48.
Figure 4.48: Symmetry boundary conditions and displacement-applied load
All nodes vertically at the end of the steel bar, marked with a black square, are equation
constrained to the ’master’ node in the bottom left corner (red node). Applying load to only
the master node, will cause the load to be distributed to the constrained nodes. The applied
load in terms of force can then be extracted as reaction force in the master node.

In Appendix C.2 the force-displacement curves for all ﬁve DSL specimen are shown. How-

decided to neglect these results. The force-displacement curves for three investigated spec-
imens can be seen in Figure 4.49.

In Table 4.22 the failure load for the three specimens are listed. The results shown in Figure

specimens had a failure load around 14-17 kN and one reached to 24.3 kN. The reason for
the rather large deviation in failure load is assumed to be because of bad manufacturing
of the specimens. The tested specimens had been previously tested to failure during this
project, but with another adhesive (Gurit). In order to reuse the specimens they had to be
grinded to remove the Gurit-adhesive. The grinding was done manually and the surfaces
of the materials (especially the GFRP) were very diﬃcult align/keep straight. This could
have had a huge eﬀect of the thickness of the applied Araldite 2015 adhesive. The average
failure load of the three specimens is 18.3 kN. using this average failure load and equation

A lot of knowledge regarding the applied cohesive laws can be extracted from the plots
shown above. Using the average shear stress obtained from experiments will correspond
to cohesive law #4. This will produce a ’failure’-load of 9.01 kN (top point of the curve)
which underestimates the experimentally obtained failure loads. The type of failure is also
stress-based and from the plot it is shown that the numerical analysis is able to converge.
The same applies for cohesive law #3. Here the failure load is 22.34 kN which is closer to
the experimental results but still this cohesive law can not be considered brittle.
In this project it is considered more correct to use a brittle cohesive law and these can be
seen are #1, #2 and #5. The diﬀerence between #1 and #2 are the critical traction stress, σc
t of
50 MPa and 40 MPa, respectively. It can be seen that the diﬀerence is negligible. The failure
load for cohesive law #2 is 36.4 kN which is approximately twice as much as the average
68
==PAGE:70==

Mads Borgnæs

failure load of 18.3 kN. The numerical failure load is 50% larger than the failure load of DSL
specimen 2 (24.3 kN). What could add to the large diﬀerence in the failure is the fact that
the FE analysis predicts symmetric stress distribution and the experiments showed non-
symmetric distribution. It is also expected that the FE model will overshoot the failure load
due to perfect interface properties compared to manufactured experimentally tested.
The size of the critical energy release rate also means a lot for determining the failure load.
As discussed in Section 4.3.4 the critical energy release rate of Mode II was extracted us-
ing a ﬁtting-function based on singular results at ﬁve phase angles. In Figure 4.51 cohesive
law #5 shows the numerical force-displacement curve of the DSL specimen with 20% re-
duced critical energy release rate compared to the obtained critical energy release rate from
the DCB-UBM tests. The failure load reduces to 32.75 kN. In this project, for investigating
the DSL and bushing specimens a cohesive law corresponding to #2 has been used. The
parameters for Mode I and Mode II are given in Table 4.23.

4.6
Root-End Bushing Tests
The ﬁnal tests conducted in the this project, was the static multi-axial tensile test of the
modiﬁed M20 root-end bushings. These test were also conducted in lab of building 119 at
DTU. The tests were carried out using a moment test-rig specially designed for multi-axial
testing of bushings. The specimens was loaded using a 500 kN Instron load cell. A sketch
of the test set-up is shown in Figure 4.54:
Figure 4.54: Skecth of bushing test conﬁguration. Pure axial test set-up (top) and multi-axial test
set-up (bottom)
In Figure 4.54, the test of a bushing is shown at pure axial tensile conﬁguration (top) and
at in an oﬀset position (bottom) which creates a clockwise bending moment during tensile
loading. From the tests it was the intension to compare the experimentally obtained surface
strains and displacements with numerical results from FE, however this was not possible
because of bad specimen manufacturing, this is further explained in Section 4.6.5.
4.6.1
Manufacturing of root-end bushing specimens
The bushings were manufactured at the DTU Risø campus. First, massive rectangular GFRP
blocks were cut out to have the size of 82x85x500 mm., see Appendix G.9. To insert the steel
bushings, a hole was drilled in both ends of the rectangular GFRP blocks. The diameter of
the drilled holes was 0.6 mm. larger than the outer diameter of the steel bushing. This was
done, as there had to be space for applying 0.3 mm. of adhesive. In Figure 4.55, the GFRP
blocks with drilled holes are shown:
71
==PAGE:73==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Figure 4.55: GFRP blocks with drilled holes
The holes were cleaned for remains after the drilling with high air pressure gun and then
cleaned with acetone. The cleaning with acetone was repeated just before inserting the steel
bushings. It should be mentioned that the inside of the holes was not grinded.
The steel bushings were fabricated according to the drawing in Appendix G.8. In Figure
4.56 the steel bushings are shown:
Figure 4.56: Steel bushings before inserted to GFRP blocks
The same surface treatment was done for steel bushings as for the other steel parts in this
project. They were cleaned with acetone until there was no dirt present. They were then
72
==PAGE:74==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
grinded in random directions using #60 sandpaper band installed to a machine. They were
then cleaned again with acetone just before applying the Araldite 2015 adhesive and in-
serted to the GFRP blocks. Since the bushings were hand-manufactured it was important
to ﬁnd a way to make sure the steel bushings could be aligned in the middle of the GFRP
holes. The ﬁrst step was to install tapered plastic plugs in the steel bushings. The large
outer diameter of the plastic plug was equal to the inner diameter of the drilled hole.
Figure 4.57: Tapered plastic plug (left) and inserted plastic plugs in bushing (right)
After installing the plastic plugs, a thick layer of Araldite 2015 adhesive was applied to the
steel to make sure there would eventually be a layer thickness of 0.3 mm. after insertion.
The steel bushings were inserted horizontally in the GFRP blocks and left for drying. The
second step in order to align the steel bushings, was inserting three pieces of 0.3 mm. steel
wires between the steel and GFRP, see sketch in Figure 4.58.
Figure 4.58: Position of steel wires for centering steel bushing in GFRP
There were two steel wires inserted at the bottom of the bushing to account for gravity
pulling the steel downwards in the drilled hole. In Figure 4.59 the ﬁve bushing specimens
are shown:
73
==PAGE:75==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Figure 4.59: The manufactured bushing specimens
For the adhesive to obtain its optimal mechanical properties, the bushings were then cured
in an oven at 70 oC.
4.6.2
Bolt Pre-Tensioning
The two inserted M20 bolts in the bushings must be pre-tensioned before the bushings are
installed in the test-rig. There exist diﬀerent types of pre-tensioning bolts, such as use of
torque wrench or hydraulic tensioning tool. Using the torque wrench method, the fric-
tion between parts comes into play. This is avoided with us of hydraulic tensioning tool.
Standards/design codes and bolted joint theories [31],[32],[33] are used in practice when
designing and analyzing bolted joints. This is needed since strain gauges are usually not
used when installing bolts. In this project, a hydraulic tensioning tool will be used and one
strain gauge will be installed on the ﬁrst bolt during pre-tension. The strain will be tracked
until the correct pre-tension is achieved. According to [31] the accuracy of pre-load is ± 1%
with use of strain gauge.
From [44],[45] the properties and dimensions for the M20 bolts were found. In Table 4.24
the used dimensions and properties for this project are shown:
Table 4.24: M20 Bolt Grade 10.9 properties and dimensions

end) M20 Fitting-Flange components designed and manufactured. One component is shown

pre-tensioned with a force of approximately 135.6 kN which corresponded to a utilization
of 76.4% at because the measured strain was done at the grinded bolt area, where the di-
ameter was 16.5 mm. 75% utilization corresponded to 133.1 kN. In Figure 4.61 the pre-load

and was intended to be used for measuring displacements and strains which could then
be compared with displacements and strains obtained numerically from a ﬁnite element
model.
The DIC cameras were put in a tilted position in order to track the displacements on the
two painted surfaces. The DIC set-up consisted of two 12 Megapixels cameras with 75 mm.

4.6.4
FE Model - M20 Root Bushing Test Specimen, 2D and 3D
Based on the previous described models, analysis’ and comparison with experimental re-
sults it has been shown how the cohesive interface elements works and what the input
parameters must be in order to obtain the most trustworthy results. From this, a 2D and
3D FE model of the root-end bushing test specimen with cohesive elements between the
steel bushing and composite material have been conducted. The purpose is to be able to
predict the pull-out strength through FE analysis. The critical load will be compared with
the experiments in order to conclude whether or not the model is valid for determining the
pull-out strength.
The M20 bushing was initially developed to respresent a Fiberline Composites A/S bush-
ing, where foam is embedded in the middle of the bushing during the pultrusion process.
Due to the self-manufactured bushings, the center part in this project is solid unidirectional
composite material. It should however be mentioned that the developed APDL-scripts are
created in way, so the center part can be modeled as any wanted material (steel, foam, com-
posite etc.)
Only 1⁄4 of the root-end bushing test specimen has been modeled in ANSYS, which can be
done due to the double symmetry plane. This is described further in Section 4.6.4.4. The
2D model of the bushing test specimen is created with use of axisymmetry. Doing this,
only pure tension can be applied. The 3D model has been modeled with a main focus on
applying eccentric load (tension in oﬀset position). This section describes how the models
work and in Appendix F.3 and Appendix F.4 the parametric APDL-scripts for the 2D model
and 3D model can be seen, respectively.
4.6.4.1
Geometry
The overall dimensions of the bushings measured 82x85x510 mm. The bushing test speci-
men dimensions and geometry can be seen in Appendix G.8. These values have been im-
plemented in APDL-scripts. The 2D axisymmetric plane model is shown in Figure 4.64 and
the 1⁄4 3D bushing is shown in Figure 4.65.
77
==PAGE:79==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Figure 4.64: Plane 2D axisymmetric FE model of bushing specimen
Figure 4.65: 3D FE model of 1⁄4 bushing specimen
When doing axisymmetric analysis in ANSYS, it is the y-axis which deﬁnes the axis of sym-
metry, which is why the longitudinal axis is modeled in the y-direction. Constructing the
bushing using axisymmetry, will give the specimen a circular cross-section, see Figure 4.66.
This means that this model can not be used to compare surface strains with the tested rect-
angular bushing. This model is intended to expect the failure load and to be used in future
fatigue studies, see Section 5 for a brief discussion regarding future works. In Table 4.26 are
the user-deﬁned dimensions listed which have been used for constructing the M20 bushing:

4.6.4.3
Elements and Meshing
For the 2D bushing, axisymmetric PLANE182 elements have been used to model the struc-
tual materials. For the interface between the GFRP and steel the cohesive interface elements
INTER202 have been used, as the number of nodes of this plane interface elements matches
the node number of PLANE182. The area of interest for the bushing is at the bondline be-
tween GFRP and steel and therefore a dense mesh is chosen here. The element length at
the bondline should be equal or less compared to the critical tangential displacement the
elements δc
t. The number of elements at the bondline can be changed by the factor n, see
Appendix F.3. The global mesh of the 2D bushing is shown in the ﬁgures shown in previ-
ous sections. In Figure 4.67 high mesh intensity is shown at the bondline between steel and
GFRP:
80
==PAGE:82==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Figure 4.67: Local mesh at bondline of 2D bushing. Cohesive elements are present, but not visible
The meshing of the 3D model is done less sophisticated, and here the user must edit the
diﬀerent meshing inputs provided in the APDL-script, see Apendix F.4. The 3D model has
been modeled using SOLID185, which is a 8-node brick element, for the structural mate-
rials. For the cohesive interface elements, INTER205 has been used, which matches the
number of nodes for SOLID185.
As previously mentioned, in ANSYS, the axisymmetic key option is deﬁned about the global
y-axis. Therefore it is important to rotate the local coordinate system of the elements (not
important for isotropic materials), so the material properties are assigned correctly with
respect to the global orientation of the model. This is implemented in the script. A lot of
eﬀort has been put into aligning the element edges and nodes towards each other. In [1] a
FE model of a bushing was investigated and it was found that diﬀerent components were
not merged correctly which lead to a wrong global stiﬀness of the structure. This has taken
into account in this updated bushing model.
4.6.4.4
Boundary Conditions & Loads
The root-end bushing test specimens have to planes of symmetry which is an advantage
regarding reducing CPU time when conducting numerical analysis. By applying symmetry
boundary conditions to these planes, the number of nodes and thereby degrees of freedom
are reduced remarkably. This is shown for the 3D bushing in Figure 4.68.
81
==PAGE:83==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Figure 4.68: Boundary conditions and loads for 3D bushing model
For applying the external tension force, a master node is used (RBE3). Using a master node,
it is possible to constrain a set of nodes, slave nodes, to this node. The external force is ap-
plied to the master node and then distributed equally to the slave nodes, which is illustrated
in Figure 4.68. The test-rig nodes at the face away from the composite have been assigned
as slave nodes. For the 3D model it possible to provide an oﬀset of the master node which
will subject the FE bushing to a bending load. Having zero oﬀset will be equal to a pure
tension load case.
For the 2D axisymmetric model the degrees of freedom are further reduced. In Figure
4.69 the symmetry boundary conditions and external loading are illustrated. For the 2D
FE model it is not possible to assign an oﬀset of the master node and only the pure tension
load case can be investigated with this model but because of axisymmetry the solution time
is much faster compared to the 3D model:
Figure 4.69: Boundary conditions and loads for 2D axisymmetric bushing model
The numerical simulation for both models is executed by having two load steps. The ﬁrst
load step is the pre-tension of the bolt and afterwards the second load step is introduced -
82
==PAGE:84==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
still the pre-tension is active in this second load step. The applying of load steps and how
they combine are shown in Figure 4.70. Each load step are applied over a time of 1 second.

4.6.4.5
Pre-tension & Contact
As shown in Figure 4.70 the bolt pre-tension is applied ﬁrst. This is done using PSMESH,
which deﬁnes elements to be pre-tensioned and in what direction the force should act. For
the 2D axisymmetric bushing it acts along the y-axis (longitudinal direction of bolt). By
applying pre-tension, the bolt will experience tension and thereby contact pressures will
arise between nut/test-rig and steel part/test-rig. The connection between bolt and thread
has not been modeled in detail. The connectivity is obtained simply by merging the bolt
elements to the steel (thread) elements.
The pre-tension implemented in the FE model is the same as the one obtained experimen-
tally, which is 135.6 kN. In order to verify that the pre-tension acts correctly in the model a
simple analytical calculation is done. A M20 Grade 10.9 bolt, with a stress area of 245 mm2,
the normal stress in the bolt at pre-tension of 135.6 kN will be:
σy,bolt = FPT
Abolt
= 135.6 kN
245 mm2 = 553.5 MPa
(4.17)
A contour plot from ANSYS of the axial (y-direction) stresses in the bushing is shown in
Figure 4.71.
83
==PAGE:85==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Figure 4.71: Contour plot of axial stresses [MPa] (y-direction) in the root bushing - Pre-tension of
135.6 kN (Load Step 1)
The plot in Figure 4.71 shows that the stress in the bolt in the range of 553.5-554.5 MPa. The
implementation of the pre-tension is concluded to work as intended. From the plot it is
also shown that stresses does not develop continually from steel to GFRP. This is expected
as the two materials have diﬀerent stiﬀness’. In Figure 4.71 the pre-tension is shown for the
2D model but the same applies for the 3D model. The highest absolute stresses are found at
the merged connection of the bolt- and steel (thread) elements but are not considered true
as the threaded connection is not modeled in detail and stress singularities arises here.
The contact pressure between steel and test-rig is also checked analytically to see that the
created contact pairs are implemented correctly. A simple way to check the contact pressure
is to distribute the pre-tension load to the area of the steel part acting with the test-rig. The
area of the steel is calculated as:

The contact stresses at the steel bushing pressing against the test-rig (left side of Figure

simple formulation assuming constant pressure at the steel area. The numerical simulation
shows that the contact pressure increases from the outer part to the inner part. The contact
pressure from the numerical simulation is within an acceptable range of the analytically
calculated and the contact pairs are validated.
The contact status is also checked. Contact pairs has been implemented between nut and
test-rig and test-rig and steel bushing. In Figure 4.73 the contact status for the 3D model is
shown during a pre-tension of 135.6 kN:
Figure 4.73: Contact status of 3D bushing at pre-tension of 135.6 kN (Load Step 1)
It is seen that total surface area of the steel part of bushing is sticking to the test-rig. The
same situation applies for contact status between the nut and test-rig. Here there is also
near contact present at the circumference of the nut. In Figure 4.74 the contact status of the
model is shown during load case 2. Here an external tension force of 80 kN is applied at a
10 mm oﬀset, which provides bending to the bushing:
85
==PAGE:87==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs

Figure 4.74: Contact status of 3D bushing at 80 kN external tension with 10 mm oﬀset (Load Step 2)
In Figure 4.74 the contact status for the global deﬂected model during the described load is
shown. Also a zoomed view of the contact pairs is shown. It is seen that the contact status
gradually changes from sticking to near contact between the steel bushing and test-rig. This
is also expected and by increasing the moment (by oﬀset or load increase) it is expected that
at some point the contact status would be far open meaning that there would be no contact
between test-rig and steel bushing in the bottom area. At the same time the contact pressure
would increase at the top area. This brief analysis shows that the contact pairs created in
this model works as intended.

master node. To validate that the external loading is applied correctly, a simple analysis is
done for both the 2D and 3D model. For the 2D model an external load of 130 kN at 0 mm.

4.6.5
Results: M20 Bushing
Five root-end bushing specimens were tested in this project. The initial plan was to test
two specimens in pure tension and then move on and test the remaining three bushings at
diﬀerent oﬀset position - multi-axial load case. During the pure tension tests it was discov-
ered that there were problems regarding the manufacturing of the specimens, as the failure
loads were very small. It was then decided to test all ﬁve specimens in pure tension to see
if the ’small’ failure load was the same for all of them. In Table 4.28 the measured failure
load for all ﬁve specimens is listed:
Table 4.28: Failure load of bushing specimens
Bushing specimen
#1
#2
#3
#4
#5
Failure load [kN]
70.38
36.42
33.08
36.50
40.80
The ﬁrst specimen had a failure load which was twice as high (approximately 70 kN) as the
remaining specimens (approximately 35 kN). For all ﬁve cases, the steel bushing suddenly
got pulled out from the composite at the failure load. In Figure 4.78 the failure of specimen
1 and 3 is shown. All ﬁve failed bushing specimens are shown in Appendix D.2:
Figure 4.78: Failed bushings, #1 (left) and #3 (right)
It was expected that the bushings would fail as they did during experiments but the low
failure load was not expected. To investigate the reason, two of the bushings (#1 and #2)
were cut open at the failed ends. The opened bushing specimen #1 and #2 are shown with
notations in Figure 4.79 and Figure 4.80, repectively:
89
==PAGE:91==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Figure 4.79: Opened bushing #1 at failed end
Figure 4.80: Opened bushing #2 at failed end
From the photos above it is very clear that a lot of the adhesive applied to the steel dur-
ing insertion, did not interact with the composite during curing. This meant the interface
strength had been reduced a lot. The insertion of the steel bushing was done manually and
it seems that much of the adhesive was taken away at the start point leaving an insuﬃcient
thickness for the remaining length of the bushing. Specimen #1 had more ’good areas’ of
connection than specimen #2 which is why the failure load for specimen #1 was higher. The
other bushing specimens were not cut open but it is assumed that the distribution of adhe-
sive would be similar to the ones presented here.
It was intended to compare the DIC measured displacements and strains of the bushing
90
==PAGE:92==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
surfaces and compare them with displacements and strains of the FE model. These in-
vestigations should have worked as a reliability check of the FE model. Due to the bad
steel-GFRP connection and low failure load, the surface strains and displacements were ap-
proximately zero and it was not possible to track any strains along the surfaces. In Figure
4.81 the DIC measured longitudinal strains for bushing #4 are shown at initial-, during- and
at failure load.
Figure 4.81: X-Strain (range -0.07% to 0.07%) of Bushing #4 at initial (top), during (middle) and
failure (bottom) load
From the plots above it is very clear that the DIC measured surface strains can not be com-
pared with results from FE. The top plot shows that the DIC was capable of tracking the
dotted-pattern surface, meaning the way of applying the dotted-pattern was okay. There
were only a few small areas which could not be tracked. Another reason for small (approx-
imately zero) surface strains could be the amount of composite-material compared to the
M20 steel bushings. Having too much composite surrounding the steel bushing will reduce
the strains at the surface.
Using the developed 2D FE model with cohesive law #2 applied to the cohesive interface
elements suggest that failure should not occur at 35 kN, 70 kN and even at 125 kN (close
to pre-tension). The cohesive elements in the FE model creates a sort of interface-surface
between the steel and GFRP, see ﬁgure below:
91
==PAGE:93==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Figure 4.82: Cohesive interface elements as a surface between steel and GFRP, 2D model
The XY-shear stress distribution of 2D bushing FE model (steel and GFRP) is shown in
Figure 4.83 together with shear stress distribution of the cohesive elements during load
case 2 where an external tension force of 125 kN is applied.
Figure 4.83: Contour plot of XY-stress [MPa] of steel and GFRP at 125 kN tension, Cohesive inter-
face elements as a surface between steel and GFRP (Load Step 2), 2D model (top) and XY-stress of
cohesive elements (bottom)
From Figure 4.83 it is seen that the absolute maximum shear stresses occurs at the ends
(marked with red circles). At the described load case with the implemented cohesive el-
ements, the FE model predicts that the root-end bushing can resist a static tensile load of
125 kN. Based on this analysis it should not be possible to reach the static failure load with-
92
==PAGE:94==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
out pulling more than the pre-tension load (135.6 kN in this project), which is not recom-
mended. As suggestion, bolts of higher grade (12.9) could be used together with higher
pre-tension utilization closer to the yield stress of the bolt.
4.6.5.1

which is just below the yield strength of the bolt. The simulation is conducted using 8% of
the DCB-UBM measured critical energy release rates:
GIc = 550 J/m2 ·0.08 = 44.0 J/m2
(4.26)

(4.27)
