5
Further Discussion & Future Works
During this project, many experimental tests and numerical analyses have been conducted
to get a better understanding the steel-GFRP bi-material interface properties and what hap-
pens in a root-bushing test specimen during loading. The results found in the individual
sections have already been discussed and this section provides extra discussion and per-
spective of the diﬀerent methods used and decisions taken in the project. This project has
only given an initial investigation of the bushing behavior during loading and a lot of work
should be done in order to fully understand the structural mechanism. Suggested future
works are also provided.
The materials used in this project were structural steel (isotropic) S355 and unidirectional
(transverse isotropic) GFRP composite. Only certain material properties of the GFRP were
tested (Ex, νxy = νxz and Gxy = Gxz). The remaining properties were assumed to be the
same as the ones from an earlier project [1], since the same company, Fiberline Composites
A/S, had produced and delivered the GFRP material. Fiberline Composites A/S had since
modiﬁed the glass-ﬁber types and therefore only the stated material properties were tested,
as the other parameters were assumed to be resin-dominated and therefore the same as
previous. For a more precise deﬁnition of the GFRP material the remaining stiﬀness prop-
erties (Ey, Ez, νyz and Gyz) should be tested. The structural steel was not tested either but
assumed to have standard values of Young’s modulus and Poisson’s ratio. It is believed that
these assumptions are valid and testing for these properties would be too time consuming
For extracting the critical energy release rates the cohesive laws of the bi-material DCB-UBM
specimens, a newly designed hydraulic rotation-controlled test machine located at DTU was
used. There exists no standard test-method for this machine and during this project it has
been a ’trial-and-error’ process. All the tests were done at speed of 1.0 deg./min. and for
future tests of brittle bi-material interfaces it is suggested to test at lower speeds for mo-
ment ratios producing almost pure Mode I conditions. Due to knowledge of the machine
had to be gained ﬁrst, only ﬁve DCB-UBM specimens were tested with installed DIC. These
ﬁve specimens were tested at diﬀerent moment ratios providing diﬀerent phase angles. A
ﬁtting-curve was ﬁtted to the obtained results and the critical energy release rates for Mode
I (Ψ = 0o) and Mode II (Ψ = 90o) could then be determined. To better determine the trend-
line from Mode I to Mode II multiple tests should be conducted at each phase angle and
also more phase angles should be tested, so more than ﬁve are investigated. It would be
very interesting to test at phase angles closer to 0 degrees and 90 degrees to see if the dat-
apoints would ﬁt the obtained ﬁtting-curve in this project. By having multiple tests at the
same phase angle would thereby make it possible to determine the standard deviation and
a lower-, best- and upper limit ﬁtting curves could be conducted as seen in [2], [3]. This
will help understanding if any over- and/or under estimation of the found critical energy
release rates in this project has occurred.
It was not possible to match the experimentally obtained phase angles based on sliding
and opening at crack tip using DIC with the phase angles from the numerical simulations.
Diﬀerent reasons could be the cause. For once, measuring the sliding and opening at the
crack tip, the rigid body motion of the DCB-UBM specimen must taken in to account. Since
it was the ﬁrst time working with DIC, and the software from GOM, it is still uncertain if this
rigid body motion were treated correctly. In this project the ﬁrst stage (picture) was used
96
==PAGE:98==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
as ’reference’ point/stage. For future tests a manually installed reference point should be
installed, which then can be used to treat the rigid body motion correctly. Another reason
for not being able to match the phase angles is thought to be the brittle fracture and also
uncertainty of the actual location of pre-crack tip. The numerical obtained phase angles
were then trusted and used.
The interface between the steel and GFRP was numerically modeled using cohesive zone
modeling. Pre-deﬁned ANSYS interface elements (INTER202 and INTER205) were used for
the diﬀerent models with attached bilinear cohesive laws. IN ANSYS, there are two ways
of implementing cohesive zone modeling; interface elements or by using always bonded con-
tact. A study could be conducted to check the diﬀerence of the two methods. In addition
to that, he exponential cohesive law shape could be investigated. A more detailed and so-
phisticated cohesive interface element could be developed through a user-deﬁned element.
It would be recommended, that if a user-deﬁned interface element is to be developed it
should be available for numerical fatigue analysis. The pre-deﬁned interface elements do
not work for fatigue analysis and at the moment it is not possible to simulate the failure of
2D and 3D bushing in fatigue in ANSYS using pre-deﬁned cohesive interface elements.
To assist the DCB-UBM tests, a FE analysis were developed to determine the phase angles
at tested moment ratios and conﬁgurations. The model calculates the Mode I and Mode II
energy release rates, and then the user can extract these values and determine the phase
angle from the reduced form. The FE model has been created using APDL-script and one
analysis can easily be conducted. However, for optimization, the script could be updated to
automatically calculate phase angles for the 6 loading conﬁguration at moment ratio 1/-1 to
-10/10 (a total of 60 load cases). The FE model can also be used in study dealing with ﬁnd-
ing an analytical formula of method for determine the phase angle of diﬀerent bi-materials
and sandwich specimens with interfacial cracks. A modiﬁed DCB-UBM FE model, where
the loading is done through displacement (rotation) instead of force (moment). With this
kind of model it will be possible to make moment-rotation plots and compare with experi-
mentally obtained moment-rotation plots from DCB-UBM tests.
The DSL tests conducted in this project were not performed under optimal conditions, as the
specimens had previously been tested to failure (with Gurit adhesive applied). Due to lack
of time it was decided to grind the failed specimen to remove the Gurit-adhesive and then
apply Araldite 2015, after surface cleaning. The grinding was done manually and for the
GFRP it was very diﬃcult to control and make sure of equal surface alignment. This could
have big inﬂuence on the failure load of each specimen. The force-displacement curves are
also very noisy and the data points ’jump’ from side to side during loading. It is not clear
why this were the case and if it could have something to do with bad re-used specimens.
The tests were conducted with a speed of 0.2 mm/min and it is suggested to reduce the
speed. It is recommended to test new DSL specimens made from newly cut steel and GFRP
pieces. If it is decided to test new DSL specimens, which has to be compared with FE-model
for this project, it is important to use the same type of surface treatment as described herein.
The ﬁnal experiments in this project were the static tensile tests of the modiﬁed root-end
bushing specimens. The initial plan was to test two bushing in pure tension and then test
three specimens at diﬀerent eccentric loading conditions. It was discovered during testing,
that the manufacturing process of the modiﬁed bushings had not been the best. All bush-
97
==PAGE:99==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
ings were then tested in pure tension (zero oﬀset) as it did not make sense to test at eccentric
loading condition before the pure tension case had been fully investigated and understood.
The reason for the low failure loads of the bushings, has been assigned to the manufac-
turing process, where adhesive was applied the steel bushings. The steel bushings were
then inserted into the drilled holes of the composite blocks manually. This procedure gives
a lot uncertainties regarding alignment of steel bushing and adhesive thickness. From cut-
ting the failed specimen it was seen, that for most of the interface surface there had been
no connection between the two materials and that most of the adhesive were located at the
edge of the drilled hole.
For new bushing specimens in the future, adhesive should also be applied inside of the
composite hole and, if possible, the inside surface of the composite should also be grinded.
A huge diﬃculty for the bushing specimens is to clamp/pressurize the materials towards
each other (like it was done for DCB-UBM and DSL) and hereby making sure the adhesive
gets in contact with both surfaces. A rather complicated solution could be to cool the steel
part so it would contract. Then apply adhesive (to both steel and composite) and insert the
steel. As the steel temperature will increase, the steel will then expand and press towards
the composite wall.
The DIC measurements for the ﬁve bushing specimens showed that there were zero sur-
face strains. The main reason for that was due to the weak connection between steel and
composite mentioned above. Another reason could be the amount of composite material.
To increase the chance for obtaining surface strains and displacements from DIC, smaller
cross-sectional area for the composite could be used. In this project cross-sectional dimen-
sions were 85x82 mm. The outer diameter of the steel bushing was 39 mm., meaning the
smallest thicknesses of composite material were 23 mm. and 21.5 mm. If new composite
blocks are to be ordered from Fiberline Composites A/S, dimensions of 65x65 mm. could
be a possibility.
98
==PAGE:100==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
5.1
Future Works Summary
In general, it would be very interesting, to use the whole ’invesitgation’-procedure (experi-
ments and FE analysis) provided in this thesis, to analyze steel-GFRP interfaces and bush-
ings manufactured using a high technological production line, as the pultrusion process
performed at Fiberline Composites A/S. A summary of the discussed above and what is
suggested to investigate in order to gain more knowledge of the structural integrity of the
bushings are listed below:
• Conduct more DCB-UBM tests. Multiple tests at same phase angle.
• Manually introduce reference point for measuring sliding and opening with DIC.
• Conduct DSL tests with new steel and composite parts.
• Find new manufacturing process for making the modiﬁed bushings
• Test valid bushing specimens and compare potential failure load and surface strains
and displacements with FE models.
• Test and fully understand the bushings in pure tension. Move on to eccentric static
load, pure tension fatigue and then eccentric fatigue tests.
• Develop user-deﬁned interface elements which can be used for numerical fatigue anal-
ysis.
99
==PAGE:101==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
6
Conclusion
Throughout this project a large range of methodologies within the mechanical engineering
ﬁeld have been used; analytical-, numerical- and experimental investigations. The main fo-
cus has been to get a better understanding of the structural integrity and failure mode of a
wind turbine blade root-end bushing. This project has provided a successful procedure for
analyzing and investigating a root-end bushing test specimen, starting from material char-
acterization of uni-directional GFRP composite to developing a full scale FE model, using
CZM, of the root-end bushing for simulating static test - in pure tension loading and eccen-
tric loading condition with ability to predict failure load and initiation of failure. All speci-
mens used in this project have been manually manufactured and working with adhesively
connected bi-material specimens it is very important to ensure good surface preparations
to achieve the best connection. This project provides a lot of new knowledge of the how
the interface behavior between steel and composite can be analyzed and used for the root-
end bushings. The interfacial fracture toughness as function of Mode-Mixity, of adhesively
bonded steel and GFRP, were succesfully measured and deﬁned using a newly DTU devel-
oped hydraulic test-rig (DCB-UBM test-rig). Working with the DCB-UBM test-rig a lot of
knowledge has also been obtained regarding how the machine operates and what should
be taken in to account when testing which can be used for future bi-material (homogenous
material) testing. The deﬁned fracture toughness for Mode I and Mode II were used to
deﬁne cohesive laws to simulate the interface between GFRP and steel during numerical
simulations. Five cohesive laws were proposed and through intermediate testing of dou-
ble shear lap specimens, the most valid cohesive law was found to simulate the interface
for root-end bushings. The developed parametric bushing FE model (2D and 3D) has been
validated through comparison with analytical formulations. Unfortunately, the manufac-
tured root-end bushing specimens were badly manufactured and provided untrustworthy
results that could not be compared with numerical simulations. It is intended, that the test
procedures and developed FE models in this project can be used, and further updated, to
predict failure of a root-end bushing subjected to cyclic loading (fatigue). The conclusions
throughout this project is chronologically brieﬂy presented in this following sections.
The material characterization tests of GFRP were conducted according to ASTM standards
and it was found the longitudinal stiﬀness of the material had decreased compared to the
material used in a previous project. Fiberline Composites A/S had also stated that with
their modiﬁcation of glass-ﬁbers, the longitudinal stiﬀness was expected to decrease.
A DCB-UBM FE model of steel and GFRP has been developed. This model was used to
extract the energy release rates at diﬀerent Mode-Mixities. The energy release rates were
extracted using the VCCT method provided by ANSYS. To validate the results, a mesh sen-
sitivity analysis was conducted and it was found that the element size at the crack tip had
to be equal of less than 0.03 mm. The numerical obtained energy release rates were also
checked and validated towards two analytical methods of obtaining the energy release rates:
J-integral value for multilayered DCB-UBM specimen and G-value of a bi-material speci-
men. All three methods were compared at diﬀerent loading conditions and since the an-
alytical calculations matched the numerical analysis, it was concluded that the DCB-UBM
(VCCT) results (energy release rates and phase angles) could be trusted.
Five DCB-UBM specimens were tested with use of DIC and the it was concluded that the
100
==PAGE:102==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
fracture process was brittle and no ﬁber bridging was present. A ﬁtting curve was ﬁtted by
eye to the measured Mixed-Mode critical energy release rates and the Mode I and Mode II
critical energy release rate were determined to be GIc = 550 J/m2 and GIIc = 1740.8 J/m2,
which are in the range of previously found critical energy release rates of adhesively bonded
joints. Five Mode I and Mode II cohesive laws were proposed to simulate the interface. In-
serting these laws in the DCB-UBM FE model indicated that the found critical energy release
rates were correct but not which law was more correct regarding fracture process. The ﬁve
proposed was used to simulate the failure of double shear lap (DSL) specimens.
Three DSL tests were performed. One specimen had a failure load of 24.3 kN and two
of them had a failure load of around 15 kN. It was also brittle failure of the DSL specimens
and a brittle cohesive law was decided to be the most correct one. This cohesive law was
implemented to a DSL FE model and predicted a failure load of 36.4 kN. The reason for
not matching results were due to non-symmetric failure of experiments, possible overes-
timation of Mode II critical energy release rate, and due to re-used specimen pieces. The
cohesive law determined based on the DSL specimens were then also used for the bushing
simulations.
Five modiﬁed M20 bushing test specimens were tested using a DTU constructed multi-axial
- all in pure tension. During the tests, it was concluded that the specimens had been badly
manufactured. Two of the tested specimens were cut open and here it was concluded that,
for a large amount of area, the adhesive layer had not interacted with both GFRP and steel,
and thereby making a weak connection. A detailed FE model of the M20 bushing has been
developed, which uses cohesive interface elements between the steel and GFRP. A thorough
presentation and variﬁcation of the model has been provided. Based on this model together
with the obtained cohesive law, it should not be possible to pull-out the steel bushing at a
load smaller load than a pre-tension of 135.6 kN. An extra FE analysis of a bushing with
much lower Mode I and Mode II cohesive laws has been conducted to show how the failure
mode and debonding of the bushing is predicted.

D
Pre-tensioning
During the installation of the bushing specimens it was expected that the pre-tensioning
had to be done in two or more steps in order to achieve the actual pre-tensioning in the
bolt. This is because of the ﬂexibility of all present components in the bolted joint. The
root-end bushing specimens was utilized by ﬁrst applying pre-tension to the inserted bolts
at each end of the specimen. The inserted M20 bolts were, as mentioned in Section 4.2.1, of
grade 10.9. From [44],[45] the properties and dimensions for the M20 bolts were found. In
Table D.1 the used dimensions and properties for this project are shown:
Table D.1: M20 Bolt Grade 10.9 properties and dimensions
Properties
Symbol
Value
Pitch diameter
d2
18.376 mm
Minor diameter
d3
16.933 mm
Stress area
Abolt
245 mm2
Proof stress
σy,bolt
830 MPa
Tensile stress
σu,bolt
1040 MPa
In Table D.1 the cross section area of the bolt is calculated based on the average value of d2
and d3:
Abolt = π ·
 d2
2 + d3
2
2
!2
= 244.8 mm2 ≈245 mm2
(D.1)
To ensure correct pre-tension load of the bolts, one strain gauge was installed in the longi-
tudinal direction to one of the bolts, see Figure D.1. in order to install the strain gauge, a
certain area of the bolt had to be grinded to achieve a smooth surface.
Figure D.1: M20 bolt for bushing test with installed strain gauge
The pre-tension was done using a mechanical hydraulic tension tool. It was ﬁrst intended
to use a torque wrench but this method turned out to be a bad choise as the specimen
started to rotate while torqueing the bolt. With use of the hydraulic tension tool the bolt
was elongated and the nut was able to be hand-tightened. Releasing the hydraulic pressure
would the yield to a pre-tension force. This way no rotation of the specimen occured and
only required a minimum of one person. The pre-tension set-up is shown in Figure D.2.
123
==PAGE:125==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Figure D.2: Pre-tension set-up
In order to use the hydraulic tension tool with additional components for the M20 bolts,
two ’transformer’ components were designed and manufactured since the test-rig was con-
structed to ﬁt M33 bolts [1]. The M20 Fitting-Flange component is shown in Figure D.3 and
the drawing can be seen in Appendix G.10.
Figure D.3: M20 Fitting Flange to be inserted into Moment-Rig to convert from M33 to M20
Here it is concluded that the bolts had to be pre-tensioned in 3 steps. In Table D.2 the pre-
load steps is shown. The wanted applied pre-tension was chosen to be 75% of the proof
124
==PAGE:126==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
strength of the bolt. The pre-tension force is calculated as:
FPT = 0.75·σy,bolt ·Abolt
FPT = 0.75·830 MPa·245 mm = 152512 N = 152.5 kN
(D.2)
The pretension force determined in (D.2) is valid for a M20 bolt without any grinded area/re-
moving of threads. Since the strain gauge was installed on the grinded smoothened area,
the measured strain and pre-tension force at 75% utilization had to be known. Because the
area of the smoothened area is less than the average M20 bolt cross section area, the mea-
sured pre-tension force is expected to be less than 152.5 kN. The diameter at grinded area
of the bolt was measured to be 16.5 mm. given an area and thereby a pre-tension force at
75% utilization of:
Abolt,test = π ·
16.5 mm
2
2
= 213.8 mm
FPT,test = 0.75·σy,bolt ·Abolt,test
= 0.75·830 MPa·201.06 mm2 = 133.1 kN
(D.3)
The pre-tension force from (D.3) was the one which was put as target for tests. The installed
strain gauge provided a strain which was then converted to force by the following linear
expression obtained from structural mechanics:
FPT,test = EAbolt,test ·ϵPT,test
(D.4)
Here E is the Young’s modulus of the bolt. The pre-tension force is shown as function of
strain in Figure D.4. The target strain is shown at a pre-tensioning of 133.1 kN.
Strain, ǫPT [-]
×10-3

133.1 kN Pre-tension at ǫPT = 0.002964
Figure D.4: Pre-tension force as function of strain in the investigated M20 bolt
For obtaining the correct pre-tension the tensioning had to be done in multiple. This was
due the ﬂexibility of the whole connection as all present materials were elastic. The loading
sequence was made in 3 steps including the unloading. The procedure was to apply pres-
sure using the hydraulic tensioning tool until the wished strain was achieved. At this point,
the nut was tightened and the pressure value was recorded before unloading. In Table D.2
125
==PAGE:127==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
the load sequence performed to obtain the correct pre-tensioning is listed. To convert the
output voltage to strain the following formula was used:
ϵPT =
4·Vout
K ·Vin ·N
(D.5)
Where Vin is the ingoing voltage of the system which were 5 V. K is the gauge factor which
is listed for each batch of ordered strain gauges. The gauge factor for this strain gauge was
2.04. N is the number of mounted strain gauges, which was 1.

From Table D.2 it is shown that the ﬁnal pre-tension was slightly above the target utilization
of 75%. However, it was very close and the same pre-tension procedure was done each
time for every bolt. That meant applying the pre-tension in three steps: 190, 200 and 210
bar. In Figure D.5 and D.6 the strain development and force development are shown. The
Datalogger was set to measure strain and at time 200 s., approximately, the ﬁrst load step
was executed. It reaches a top point and afterwards it decreases slowly until the pressure of
the hydraulic pressure released was released, given a sharp drop. This behaviour applies
for all three load steps. The slow drop was because the hydraulic pump was not totally tight
and leaked a bit. The sharp drop was due to the ﬂexibility of the compressed parts of the
bolted joint parts.

E.2
Energy release rate, G, for bi-material
Listing 2: G of bi-material, GFRP-Steel
1
%Master Thesis
2
%Interface Cracking Between 2 Elastic Orthotropic Layers

%Mads Borgnaes
4
%DTU −23/8−17
5
6
%% Phase Angle of GFRP−Steel specimen ('Mode I')
7
8
close all
9
clear all
10
clc
11
12
% Material 1# = Steel
13
% Material 2# = GFRP
14
15
%Material inputs
16
width = 40;
%Width of specimen
17
h1 = 10;
h2 = 10;
%Thickness of layers [mm]
18
Ex1 = 31606; Ex2 = 210000; %Elastic moduli x [MPa]
19
Ey1 = 11000;
Ey2 = Ex2; %Elastic moduli y [MPa]
20
Ez1 = 11000;
Ez2 = Ex2; %Elastic moduli z [MPa]
21
vxy1 = 0.34;
vxy2 = 0.3; %Poisson's ratio xy [−] Major
22
vxz1 = 0.34;
vxz2 = vxy2; %Poisson's ratio xz [−] Major
23
vyz1 = 0.45;
vyz2 = vxy2; %Poisson's ratio yz [−] Major
24
Gxy1 = 3443;
Gxy2 = Ex2/(2*(1+vxy2^2)); %Shear moduli xy [−] Major
25
Gxz1 = 3443;
Gxz2 = Gxy2; %Shear moduli xz [−] Major
26
Gyz1 = Ey1/(2*(1+vyz1^2));
Gyz2 = Gxy2; %Shear moduli yz [−] Major
27
28
vzx1 = (Ez1*vxz1)/Ex1; vzx2 = (Ez2*vxz2)/Ex2;
%Shear moduli zx [−] Minor
29
Ex1s = Ex1/(1−vzx1*vxz1); Ex2s = Ex2/(1−vzx2*vxz2);
30
31
%Non−dimensional parameters
32
eta = h1/h2;
33
Sigma = Ex1s/Ex2s;
34
Delta = (1+2*eta*Sigma+eta^2*Sigma)/(2*eta*(1+eta*Sigma));
35
beta = Delta−1/eta;
36
A0 = 1/eta+Sigma;
37
I0 = (1/3)*(Sigma*(3*beta^2−3*beta+1)+3*(Delta/eta)*beta+(1/eta^3));
38
39
C1 = (Sigma/A0);
40
C2 = (Sigma/(h1*I0))*(1/2−beta);
41
C3 = Sigma/(12*I0);
42
43
A = 1/(1+Sigma*(4*eta+6*eta^2+3*eta^3));
44
Ii = 1/(12*(1+Sigma*eta^3));
45
gamma = asin(6*Sigma*eta^2*(1+eta)*sqrt(A*Ii));

! Model: Geometry, Materials, Meshing
/PREP7
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Dimensions ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
!Bushing
L=500+2*5 !Length of specimen [mm]
contact=5 !Extrusion of steel for contact [mm]
Ltap=100.00-contact !Length up to tapered part [mm]
tap=55.00 !Length of tapering [mm]
tc=23.00 !Thickness of composite [mm]
Lfree=27.50 !Length of tapering part before foam/composite [mm]
thin=0.1 !Thin end of steel and composite [mm]
bolt_hole=22.00 !Bolt hole diameter (M20) [mm]

==PAGE:155==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
od_steel=39.00 !Outer diameter of steel [mm]
h=od_steel+2*tc !Total height specimen [mm]
b=82.00 !Width of specimen [mm]
!Bolt and thread in bushing
thread_dis=50.00-contact !Distance to thread [mm]
L_thread=48.00 !Length of thread [mm]
bolt_od=17.6545 !Diameter of bolt (M20) [mm] ((d2+d3)/2)
thread_id=bolt_od !Inner diameter of thread [mm]
nut_od=39.26 !Nut OD [mm]
nut_h=20.7 !Nut height [mm]

!Test-rig piece
thick=100 !Thickness of test-rig piece [mm]
ex_test=30
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Loads ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
pre_tension=135.6 !Pretension of bolt [kN]
Tension=130 !Applied tension [kN]
offset=0 !Offset of tension load [mm]
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Contact ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
fric=0.2 !Friction coefficient steel-steel [-]
penalty=1 !Penalty Stiffness [-]
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Meshing inputs ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤

!Factor of elements horizontal before tapering [-]30

!Factor of elements horizontal for tapering approx 1/4 of elements before t

!Number of elements tapering [-]5

Elem_thin_c=1 !Number of elements at thin composite end [-]1

x=10 !Ratio of elements [-]

method=1 !Axisymmetric keyoption [-]
qwe=1/1000 !For choosing nodes [-]
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ MATERIALS ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Composite properties ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
Exx=31606 !Young’s Modulus in x-direction [MPa] !38160
Eyy=11000 !Young’s Modulus in y-direction [MPa] !11000

pxy=0.34 !Poisson’s ratio in xy-plane [-] !0.33

Mads Borgnæs

pxz=0.34 !Poisson’s ratio in xz-plane [-]
Gxy=3443 !Shear Modulus in xy-plane [MPa] !5000
Gyz=3790 !Shear Modulus in yz-plane [MPa] !3790
Gxz=3443 !Shear Modulus in xz-plane [MPa]
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ CZ law properties ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
G1c=0.55 !Critical Energy Release Rate Mode I [N/mm] 0.613
G2c=1.740 !Critical Energy Release Rate Mode II [N/mm] 2.252
Nmax=5.000 !Maximum normal traction [MPa] !12
Smax=15.000 !Maximum tangential traction [MPa]!22
PenaltyK=900.0 !Stiffness of load-displacement curve
PenaltyKI=1000.0 !Stiffness of load-displacement curve
PenaltyKII=1000.0 !Stiffness of load-displacement curve
beta=1.000 !Non-dimensional weighting parameter
! Calculating Material parameters CZM-element
delta_n_0 = Nmax/PenaltyK !Normal displacement jump at maximum normal cohesive traction
delta_n_c = G1c/(0.5*Nmax)
!Normal displacement jump at the completion of debonding
delta_t_0 = Smax/PenaltyK !Tangential displacement jump at maximum normal cohesive tracti
delta_t_c = G2c/(0.5*Smax) !Tangential displacement jump at the completion of debonding
alpha=(delta_n_0/delta_n_c+delta_t_0/delta_t_c)/2 !Average of the two ratios since only o
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Steel properties ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
Esteel=210000 !Young’s Modulus [MPa]
psteel=0.3 !Poisson’s ratio [-]
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Insert properties ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
Eplas=210000 !Young’s Modulus [MPa]
pplas=0.3 !Poisson’s ratio [-]
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Bolt properties ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤

pbolt=0.3 !Poisson’s ratio [-]
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Nut properties ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
Enut=210000 !Young’s Modulus [MPa]
pnut=0.3 !Poisson’s ratio [-]
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Test rig properties ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
Etest=210000 !Young’s Modulus [MPa]
ptest=0.3 !Poisson’s ratio [-]

M20 Root-end bushing (3D)
!%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

Mads Borgnæs

Root-End Bushing w. Plastic

FINISH
keyw,pr_sgui,1 ! Make sure that the verify box "are you sure you want to /clear.." does n
/UIS,MSGPOP,3 ! only show error messages
/CLEAR
/BATCH
/TITLE,M20 Root Bushing w. Plastic
/FILNAME,m20_bushing_job.dat,0
/’X:\MasterAnsys\Scripts2\3D Bushing’
!* Set Working directory
!* Update the toolbar
*ABBR, Run Model, /INPUT,m20_bushing_job,’dat’,,, 0
*ABBR, PlotDisp, /INPUT,’PlotForceDisp’,’mac’,,, 0
*ABBR, MakeMovie, /INPUT,’MakeMovieDisp’,’mac’,,, 0
! Model: Geometry, Materials, Meshing
/PREP7
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Dimensions ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
!Bushing
L=500+2*5 !Length of specimen [mm]
contact=5 !Extrusion of steel for contact [mm]
Ltap=100.00-contact !Length up to tapered part [mm]
tap=55.00 !Length of tapering [mm]
tc=23.00 !Thickness of composite [mm]
Lfree=27.50 !Length of tapering part before foam/composite [mm]
thin=0.5 !Thin end of steel and composite [mm]
bolt_hole=22.00 !Bolt hole diameter (M20) [mm]
od_steel=39.00 !Outer diameter of steel [mm]
177
==PAGE:179==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
h=od_steel+2*tc !Total height specimen [mm]
b=82.00 !Width of specimen [mm]
!Bolt and thread in bushing
thread_dis=50.00-contact !Distance to thread [mm]
L_thread=48.00 !Length of thread [mm]
bolt_od=17.6545 !Diameter of bolt (M20) [mm] ((d2+d3)/2)
thread_id=bolt_od !Inner diameter of thread [mm]
nut_od=39.26 !Nut OD [mm]
nut_h=20.7 !Nut height [mm]
L_bolt=thread_dis+L_thread+2 !Length of bolt [mm]
!Test-rig piece
thick=100 !Thickness of test-rig piece [mm]
ex_test=30
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Loads ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
pre_tension=135.6 !Pretension of bolt [kN]
Tension=80 !Applied tension [kN]
offset=5 !Offset of tension load [mm]
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Contact ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
fric=0.2 !Friction coefficient steel-steel [-]
penalty=1 !Penalty Stiffness [-]
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Meshing inputs ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
Elem_b_tap=40
!Number of elements horizontal before tapering [-]35
Elem_h_tap=40
!Number of elements horizontal for tapering approx 1/4 of elements before
Elem_tap=8
!Number of elements tapering [-]7
Elem_s_t=3 !Number of elements through steel thickness [-]3
Elem_t_c=4 !Number of elements through composite thickness [-]4
Elem_thin_s=1 !Number of elements at thin steel end [-]1
Elem_thin_c=1 !Number of elements at thin composite end [-]1
Elem_round=8 !Number of elements in rounding [-]8
Elem_foam_t=3 !Number of elements through foam [-] 5
method=0 !0=Full integration, 1=Reduced integration, !ONLY FOR SOLID185 2=Enhanced Strain
qwe=1/1000 !For choosing nodes [-]
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ MATERIALS ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Composite properties ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
Exx=31606 !Young’s Modulus in x-direction [MPa]
Eyy=11000 !Young’s Modulus in y-direction [MPa]
Ezz=11000 !Young’s Modulus in z-direction [MPa]
pxy=0.34 !Poisson’s ratio in xy-plane [-]
pyz=0.45 !Poisson’s ratio in yz-plane [-]
pxz=0.34 !Poisson’s ratio in xz-plane [-]
Gxy=3443 !Shear Modulus in xy-plane [-]
Gyz=3790 !Shear Modulus in yz-plane [-]
178
==PAGE:180==
Master Thesis
Mads Borgnæs
2017
Gxz=3443 !Shear Modulus in xz-plane [-]
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ CZ law properties ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
G1c=0.55 !Critical Energy Release Rate Mode I [N/mm] 0.613
G2c=1.74085 !Critical Energy Release Rate Mode II [N/mm] 2.252
Nmax=25.0 !Maximum normal traction [MPa] !12
Smax=40.0 !Maximum tangential traction [MPa]!22
PenaltyK=900 !Stiffness of load-displacement curve
beta=1.000 !Non-dimensional weighting parameter
! Calculating Material parameters CZM-element
delta_n_0 = Nmax/PenaltyK !Normal displacement jump at maximum normal cohesive traction
delta_n_c = G1c/(0.5*Nmax)
!Normal displacement jump at the completion of debonding
delta_t_0 = Smax/PenaltyK !Tangential displacement jump at maximum normal cohesive tracti
delta_t_c = G2c/(0.5*Smax) !Tangential displacement jump at the completion of debonding
alpha=(delta_n_0/delta_n_c+delta_t_0/delta_t_c)/2 !Average of the two ratios since only o
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Steel properties ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
Esteel=210000 !Young’s Modulus [MPa]
psteel=0.3 !Poisson’s ratio [-]
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Plastic properties ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
Eplas=210000 !Young’s Modulus [MPa]
pplas=0.3 !Poisson’s ratio [-]
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Bolt properties ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
Ebolt=210000 !Young’s Modulus [MPa]
pbolt=0.3 !Poisson’s ratio [-]
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Nut properties ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
Enut=210000 !Young’s Modulus [MPa]
pnut=0.3 !Poisson’s ratio [-]
! ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤ Test rig properties ¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤¤
Etest=210000 !Young’s Modulus [MPa]
ptest=0.3 !Poisson’s ratio [-]
mat_comp =1
mat_steel =2
mat_plas =3
mat_bolt =4
mat_nut =5
